.navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_oxygen" title="Template:Infobox oxygen"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_oxygen" title="Template talk:Infobox oxygen"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_oxygen&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Oxygen</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_symbol" title="Chemical symbol">symbol</a> <b>O</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 8. It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">chalcogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Group_(periodic_table)" title="Group (periodic table)">group</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, a highly <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">reactive</a> <a href="/wiki/Nonmetal" title="Nonmetal">nonmetal</a>, and an <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">oxidizing agent</a> that readily forms <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> with most elements as well as with other <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a>. Oxygen is Earth's <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">most abundant element</a>, and after <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, it is the third-most abundant element in the universe. At <a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, two atoms of the element <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">bind</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_oxygen#Dioxygen" title="Allotropes of oxygen">dioxygen</a>, a colorless and odorless <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">diatomic</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> with the formula <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. Diatomic oxygen gas currently constitutes 20.95% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>, though this has <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_oxygen" title="Geological history of oxygen">changed considerably</a> over long periods of time. Oxygen makes up almost half of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a> in the form of oxides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atkins7th_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins7th-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many major classes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecule" title="Organic molecule">organic molecules</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Living_organism" title="Living organism">living organisms</a> contain oxygen atoms, such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a>, as do the major constituent <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic compounds</a> of animal shells, teeth, and bone. Most of the mass of living organisms is oxygen as a component of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, the major constituent of lifeforms. Oxygen is continuously replenished in Earth's atmosphere by <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, which uses the energy of sunlight to produce oxygen from water and carbon dioxide. Oxygen is too chemically reactive to remain a free element in air without being continuously replenished by the photosynthetic action of living organisms. Another form (<a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_oxygen" title="Allotropes of oxygen">allotrope</a>) of oxygen, <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), strongly absorbs ultraviolet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UVB" title="UVB">UVB</a> radiation and the high-altitude <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> helps protect the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a>. However, ozone present at the surface is a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Smog" title="Smog">smog</a> and thus a pollutant.
</p><p>Oxygen was isolated by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Sendivogius" title="Michael Sendivogius">Michael Sendivogius</a> before 1604, but it is commonly believed that the element was discovered independently by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Uppsala" title="Uppsala">Uppsala</a>, in 1773 or earlier, and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wiltshire" title="Wiltshire">Wiltshire</a>, in 1774. Priority is often given for Priestley because his work was published first. Priestley, however, called oxygen "dephlogisticated air", and did not recognize it as a chemical element. The name <i>oxygen</i> was coined in 1777 by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a>, who first recognized oxygen as a chemical element and correctly characterized the role it plays in combustion.
</p><p>Common uses of oxygen include production of <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxy-fuel_welding_and_cutting" title="Oxy-fuel welding and cutting">brazing, welding and cutting</a> of steels and other <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rocket_propellant" title="Rocket propellant">rocket propellant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_therapy" title="Oxygen therapy">oxygen therapy</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_support_system" title="Life support system">life support systems</a> in <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">spaceflight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Underwater_diving" title="Underwater diving">diving</a>.
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_study">History of study</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_experiments">Early experiments</span></h3>
<p>One of the first known experiments on the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> and air was conducted by the 2nd century BCE <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> writer on mechanics, <a href="/wiki/Philo_of_Byzantium" title="Philo of Byzantium">Philo of Byzantium</a>. In his work <i>Pneumatica</i>, Philo observed that inverting a vessel over a burning candle and surrounding the vessel's neck with water resulted in some water rising into the neck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Philo incorrectly surmised that parts of the air in the vessel were converted into the <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">classical element</a> <a href="/wiki/Fire_(classical_element)" title="Fire (classical element)">fire</a> and thus were able to escape through pores in the glass. Many centuries later <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> built on Philo's work by observing that a portion of air is consumed during combustion and <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">respiration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE499_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE499-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> proved that air is necessary for combustion. English chemist <a href="/wiki/John_Mayow" title="John Mayow">John Mayow</a> (1641–1679) refined this work by showing that fire requires only a part of air that he called <i>spiritus nitroaereus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-6">[6]</a></sup> In one experiment, he found that placing either a mouse or a lit candle in a closed container over water caused the water to rise and replace one-fourteenth of the air's volume before extinguishing the subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoC_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-WoC-7">[7]</a></sup> From this, he surmised that nitroaereus is consumed in both respiration and combustion.
</p><p>Mayow observed that <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a> increased in weight when heated, and inferred that the nitroaereus must have combined with it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-6">[6]</a></sup> He also thought that the lungs separate nitroaereus from air and pass it into the blood and that animal heat and muscle movement result from the reaction of nitroaereus with certain substances in the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-6">[6]</a></sup> Accounts of these and other experiments and ideas were published in 1668 in his work <i>Tractatus duo</i> in the tract "De respiratione".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoC_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-WoC-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phlogiston_theory">Phlogiston theory</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">Phlogiston theory</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ole_Borch" title="Ole Borch">Ole Borch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lomonosov" title="Mikhail Lomonosov">Mikhail Lomonosov</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Bayen" title="Pierre Bayen">Pierre Bayen</a> all produced oxygen in experiments in the 17th and the 18th century but none of them recognized it as a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB299_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBB299-8">[8]</a></sup> This may have been in part due to the prevalence of the philosophy of combustion and <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> called the <i>phlogiston theory</i>, which was then the favored explanation of those processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Established in 1667 by the German alchemist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._J._Becher" title="J. J. Becher">J. J. Becher</a>, and modified by the chemist <a href="/wiki/Georg_Ernst_Stahl" title="Georg Ernst Stahl">Georg Ernst Stahl</a> by 1731,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morris_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-morris-10">[10]</a></sup> phlogiston theory stated that all combustible materials were made of two parts. One part, called phlogiston, was given off when the substance containing it was burned, while the dephlogisticated part was thought to be its true form, or <a href="/wiki/Calx" title="Calx">calx</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE499_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-ECE499-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Highly combustible materials that leave little <a href="/wiki/Residue_(chemistry)" title="Residue (chemistry)">residue</a>, such as wood or coal, were thought to be made mostly of phlogiston; non-combustible substances that corrode, such as iron, contained very little. Air did not play a role in phlogiston theory, nor were any initial quantitative experiments conducted to test the idea; instead, it was based on observations of what happens when something burns, that most common objects appear to become lighter and seem to lose something in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE499_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-ECE499-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyFuseli.jpg"><img alt="A drawing of an elderly man sitting by a table and facing parallel to the drawing. His left arm rests on a notebook, legs crossed." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="587" data-file-width="457" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/PriestleyFuseli.jpg/170px-PriestleyFuseli.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/PriestleyFuseli.jpg/255px-PriestleyFuseli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/PriestleyFuseli.jpg/340px-PriestleyFuseli.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyFuseli.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> is usually given priority in the discovery.</div></div></div>
<p>Polish <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sendivogius" title="Sendivogius">Michael Sendivogius</a> (Michał Sędziwój) in his work <i>De Lapide Philosophorum Tractatus duodecim e naturae fonte et manuali experientia depromti</i> (1604) described a substance contained in air, referring to it as 'cibus vitae' (food of life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marples_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marples-11">[11]</a></sup>) and according to Polish historian Roman Bugaj, this substance is identical with oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bugaj_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bugaj-12">[12]</a></sup> Sendivogius, during his experiments performed between 1598 and 1604, properly recognized that the substance is equivalent to the gaseous byproduct released by the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_decomposition" title="Thermal decomposition">thermal decomposition</a> of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">potassium nitrate</a>. In Bugaj's view, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_purification_methods_in_chemistry" title="List of purification methods in chemistry">isolation</a> of oxygen and the proper association of the substance to that part of air which is required for life, provides sufficient evidence for the discovery of oxygen by Sendivogius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bugaj_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bugaj-12">[12]</a></sup> This discovery of Sendivogius was however frequently denied by the generations of scientists and chemists which succeeded him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marples_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marples-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is also commonly claimed that oxygen was first discovered by Swedish pharmacist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a>. He had produced oxygen gas by heating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercuric_oxide" title="Mercuric oxide">mercuric oxide</a> (HgO) and various <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a> in 1771–72.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE500-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE499_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-ECE499-5">[5]</a></sup> Scheele called the gas "fire air" because it was then the only known <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">agent</a> to support combustion. He wrote an account of this discovery in a manuscript titled <i>Treatise on Air and Fire</i>, which he sent to his publisher in 1775. That document was published in 1777.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBB300-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the meantime, on August 1, 1774, an experiment conducted by the British clergyman <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> focused sunlight on mercuric oxide contained in a glass tube, which liberated a gas he named "dephlogisticated air".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-ECE500-14">[14]</a></sup> He noted that candles burned brighter in the gas and that a mouse was more active and lived longer while <a href="/wiki/Breathing" title="Breathing">breathing</a> it. After breathing the gas himself, Priestley wrote: "The feeling of it to my lungs was not sensibly different from that of <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">common air</a>, but I fancied that my breast felt peculiarly light and easy for some time afterwards."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB299_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-NBB299-8">[8]</a></sup> Priestley published his findings in 1775 in a paper titled "An Account of Further Discoveries in Air", which was included in the second volume of his book titled <i><a href="/wiki/Experiments_and_Observations_on_Different_Kinds_of_Air" title="Experiments and Observations on Different Kinds of Air">Experiments and Observations on Different Kinds of Air</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE499_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-ECE499-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Because he published his findings first, Priestley is usually given priority in the discovery.
</p><p>The French chemist <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Laurent Lavoisier</a> later claimed to have discovered the new substance independently. Priestley visited Lavoisier in October 1774 and told him about his experiment and how he liberated the new gas. Scheele had also dispatched a letter to Lavoisier on September 30, 1774, which described his discovery of the previously unknown substance, but Lavoisier never acknowledged receiving it. (A copy of the letter was found in Scheele's belongings after his death.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-NBB300-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Lavoisier.27s_contribution"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lavoisier's_contribution">Lavoisier's contribution</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antoine_lavoisier.jpg"><img alt="A drawing of a young man facing towards the viewer, but looking on the side. He wear a white curly wig, dark suit and white scarf." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="318" data-file-width="303" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Antoine_lavoisier.jpg/170px-Antoine_lavoisier.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Antoine_lavoisier.jpg/255px-Antoine_lavoisier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/96/Antoine_lavoisier.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antoine_lavoisier.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> discredited the phlogiston theory.</div></div></div>
<p>Lavoisier conducted the first adequate quantitative experiments on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> and gave the first correct explanation of how combustion works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-ECE500-14">[14]</a></sup> He used these and similar experiments, all started in 1774, to discredit the phlogiston theory and to prove that the substance discovered by Priestley and Scheele was a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a>.
</p><p>In one experiment, Lavoisier observed that there was no overall increase in weight when <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> and air were heated in a closed container.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-ECE500-14">[14]</a></sup> He noted that air rushed in when he opened the container, which indicated that part of the trapped air had been consumed. He also noted that the tin had increased in weight and that increase was the same as the weight of the air that rushed back in. This and other experiments on combustion were documented in his book <i>Sur la combustion en général</i>, which was published in 1777.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-ECE500-14">[14]</a></sup> In that work, he proved that air is a mixture of two gases; 'vital air', which is essential to combustion and respiration, and <i>azote</i> (Gk. <i><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἄζωτον</span></span></i> "lifeless"), which did not support either. <i>Azote</i> later became <i><a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a></i> in English, although it has kept the earlier name in French and several other European languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-ECE500-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h4>
<p>Lavoisier renamed 'vital air' to <i>oxygène</i> in 1777 from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> roots <i><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ὀξύς</span></span> (oxys)</i> (<a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a>, literally "sharp", from the taste of acids) and <i>-γενής (-genēs)</i> (producer, literally begetter), because he mistakenly believed that oxygen was a constituent of all acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mellor_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-17">[17]</a></sup> Chemists (such as Sir <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> in 1812) eventually determined that Lavoisier was wrong in this regard, but by then the name was too well established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Oxygen</i> entered the English language despite opposition by English scientists and the fact that the Englishman Priestley had first isolated the gas and written about it. This is partly due to a poem praising the gas titled "Oxygen" in the popular book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Botanic_Garden" title="The Botanic Garden">The Botanic Garden</a></i> (1791) by <a href="/wiki/Erasmus_Darwin" title="Erasmus Darwin">Erasmus Darwin</a>, grandfather of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-NBB300-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_history">Later history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg"><img alt="A metal frame structure stands on the snow near a tree. A middle-aged man wearing a coat, boots, leather gloves and a cap stands by the structure and holds it with his right hand." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2946" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg/170px-Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg/255px-Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg/340px-Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goddard_and_Rocket.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Goddard" title="Robert H. Goddard">Robert H. Goddard</a> and a liquid oxygen-gasoline <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Dalton" title="John Dalton">John Dalton</a>'s original <a href="/wiki/Atomic_theory#Earliest_empirical_evidence" title="Atomic theory">atomic hypothesis</a> presumed that all elements were monatomic and that the atoms in compounds would normally have the simplest atomic ratios with respect to one another. For example, Dalton assumed that water's formula was HO, leading to the conclusion that the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a> of oxygen was 8 times that of hydrogen, instead of the modern value of about 16.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In 1805, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac" title="Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac">Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a> showed that water is formed of two volumes of hydrogen and one volume of oxygen; and by 1811 <a href="/wiki/Amedeo_Avogadro" title="Amedeo Avogadro">Amedeo Avogadro</a> had arrived at the correct interpretation of water's composition, based on what is now called <a href="/wiki/Avogadro%27s_law" title="Avogadro's law">Avogadro's law</a> and the diatomic elemental molecules in those gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[a]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first commercial method of producing oxygen was chemical, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Brin_process" title="Brin process">Brin process</a> involving a reversible reaction of <a href="/wiki/Barium_oxide" title="Barium oxide">barium oxide</a>. It was invented in 1852 and commercialized in 1884, but was displaced by newer methods in early 20th century.
</p><p>By the late 19th century scientists realized that air could be liquefied and its components isolated by compressing and cooling it. Using a <a href="/wiki/Cascade_(chemical_engineering)" title="Cascade (chemical engineering)">cascade</a> method, Swiss chemist and physicist <a href="/wiki/Raoul_Pictet" title="Raoul Pictet">Raoul Pierre Pictet</a> <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporated</a> liquid <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> in order to liquefy carbon dioxide, which in turn was evaporated to cool oxygen gas enough to liquefy it. He sent a telegram on December 22, 1877, to the <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">French Academy of Sciences</a> in Paris announcing his discovery of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BES707_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-BES707-22">[21]</a></sup> Just two days later, French physicist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Paul_Cailletet" title="Louis Paul Cailletet">Louis Paul Cailletet</a> announced his own method of liquefying molecular oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BES707_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-BES707-22">[21]</a></sup> Only a few drops of the liquid were produced in each case and no meaningful analysis could be conducted. Oxygen was liquefied in a stable state for the first time on March 29, 1883, by Polish scientists from <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">Jagiellonian University</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zygmunt_Wr%C3%B3blewski" title="Zygmunt Wróblewski">Zygmunt Wróblewski</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karol_Olszewski" title="Karol Olszewski">Karol Olszewski</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_setup_for_preparation_of_Oxygen.jpg"><img alt="An experiment setup with test tubes to prepare oxygen" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1206" data-file-width="2171" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/A_setup_for_preparation_of_Oxygen.jpg/280px-A_setup_for_preparation_of_Oxygen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/A_setup_for_preparation_of_Oxygen.jpg/420px-A_setup_for_preparation_of_Oxygen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/A_setup_for_preparation_of_Oxygen.jpg/560px-A_setup_for_preparation_of_Oxygen.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_setup_for_preparation_of_Oxygen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An experiment setup for preparation of oxygen in academic laboratories</div></div></div>
<p>In 1891 Scottish chemist <a href="/wiki/James_Dewar" title="James Dewar">James Dewar</a> was able to produce enough liquid oxygen for study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB303_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBB303-24">[23]</a></sup> The first commercially viable process for producing liquid oxygen was independently developed in 1895 by German engineer <a href="/wiki/Carl_von_Linde" title="Carl von Linde">Carl von Linde</a> and British engineer William Hampson. Both men lowered the temperature of air until it liquefied and then <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distilled</a> the component gases by boiling them off one at a time and capturing them separately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HPAM_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-HPAM-25">[24]</a></sup> Later, in 1901, oxyacetylene <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> was demonstrated for the first time by burning a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a> and compressed <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. This method of welding and cutting metal later became common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HPAM_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-HPAM-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1923, the American scientist <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Goddard" title="Robert H. Goddard">Robert H. Goddard</a> became the first person to develop a <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine" title="Rocket engine">rocket engine</a> that burned liquid fuel; the engine used <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> for fuel and liquid oxygen as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidizer" title="Oxidizer">oxidizer</a>. Goddard successfully flew a small liquid-fueled rocket 56 m at 97 km/h on March 16, 1926, in <a href="/wiki/Auburn,_Massachusetts" title="Auburn, Massachusetts">Auburn, Massachusetts</a>, US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HPAM_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-HPAM-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In academic laboratories, oxygen can be prepared by heating together potassium chlorate mixed with a small proportion of manganese dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Oxygen levels in the atmosphere are trending slightly downward globally, possibly because of fossil-fuel burning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_and_molecular_structure">Properties and molecular structure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oxygen_molecule_orbitals_diagram.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="959" data-file-width="963" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Oxygen_molecule_orbitals_diagram.JPG/260px-Oxygen_molecule_orbitals_diagram.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Oxygen_molecule_orbitals_diagram.JPG/390px-Oxygen_molecule_orbitals_diagram.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Oxygen_molecule_orbitals_diagram.JPG/520px-Oxygen_molecule_orbitals_diagram.JPG 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oxygen_molecule_orbitals_diagram.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Orbital diagram, after Barrett (2002),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barrett2002_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett2002-29">[28]</a></sup> showing the participating atomic orbitals from each oxygen atom, the molecular orbitals that result from their overlap, and the <a href="/wiki/Aufbau_principle" title="Aufbau principle">aufbau</a> filling of the orbitals with the 12 electrons, 6 from each O atom, beginning from the lowest-energy orbitals, and resulting in covalent double-bond character from filled orbitals (and cancellation of the contributions of the pairs of σ and σ<sup>*</sup> and π and π<sup>*</sup> orbital pairs).</div></div></div>
<p>At <a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, oxygen is a colorless, odorless, and tasteless gas with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_formula" title="Molecular formula">molecular formula</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, referred to as dioxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>As <i>dioxygen</i>, two oxygen atoms are <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemically bound</a> to each other. The bond can be variously described based on level of theory, but is reasonably and simply described as a covalent <a href="/wiki/Double_bond" title="Double bond">double bond</a> that results from the filling of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_orbitals" title="Molecular orbitals">molecular orbitals</a> formed from the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbitals</a> of the individual oxygen atoms, the filling of which results in a <a href="/wiki/Bond_order" title="Bond order">bond order</a> of two. More specifically, the double bond is the result of sequential, low-to-high energy, or <a href="/wiki/Aufbau_principle" title="Aufbau principle">Aufbau</a>, filling of orbitals, and the resulting cancellation of contributions from the 2s electrons, after sequential filling of the low σ and σ<sup>*</sup> orbitals; σ overlap of the two atomic 2p orbitals that lie along the O–O molecular axis and π overlap of two pairs of atomic 2p orbitals perpendicular to the O–O molecular axis, and then cancellation of contributions from the remaining two 2p electrons after their partial filling of the π<sup>*</sup> orbitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barrett2002_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett2002-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>This combination of cancellations and σ and π overlaps results in dioxygen's double-bond character and reactivity, and a triplet electronic <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a>. An <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a> with two unpaired electrons, as is found in dioxygen orbitals (see the filled π* orbitals in the diagram) that are of equal energy—i.e., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degenerate_orbitals" title="Degenerate orbitals">degenerate</a>—is a configuration termed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin_triplet" title="Spin triplet">spin triplet</a> state. Hence, the ground state of the <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> molecule is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Triplet_oxygen" title="Triplet oxygen">triplet oxygen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiochemOnline_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-BiochemOnline-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[b]</a></sup> The highest-energy, partially filled orbitals are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibonding" title="Antibonding">antibonding</a>, and so their filling weakens the bond order from three to two. Because of its unpaired electrons, triplet oxygen reacts only slowly with most organic molecules, which have paired electron spins; this prevents spontaneous combustion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astm-tpt_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-astm-tpt-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liquid_oxygen_in_a_magnet_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="302" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Liquid_oxygen_in_a_magnet_2.jpg/170px-Liquid_oxygen_in_a_magnet_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Liquid_oxygen_in_a_magnet_2.jpg/255px-Liquid_oxygen_in_a_magnet_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/70/Liquid_oxygen_in_a_magnet_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liquid_oxygen_in_a_magnet_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Liquid oxygen, temporarily suspended in a magnet owing to its paramagnetism</div></div></div>
<p>In the triplet form, <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> molecules are <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">paramagnetic</a>. That is, they impart magnetic character to oxygen when it is in the presence of a magnetic field, because of the <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">magnetic moments</a> of the unpaired electrons in the molecule, and the negative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exchange_energy" title="Exchange energy">exchange energy</a> between neighboring <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB303_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-NBB303-24">[23]</a></sup> Liquid oxygen is so <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">magnetic</a> that, in laboratory demonstrations, a bridge of liquid oxygen may be supported against its own weight between the poles of a powerful magnet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[c]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Singlet_oxygen" title="Singlet oxygen">Singlet oxygen</a> is a name given to several higher-energy species of molecular <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> in which all the electron spins are paired. It is much more reactive with common <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic molecules</a> than is normal (triplet) molecular oxygen. In nature, singlet oxygen is commonly formed from water during photosynthesis, using the energy of sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> It is also produced in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> by the photolysis of ozone by light of short wavelength<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harrison_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-harrison-37">[34]</a></sup> and by the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> as a source of active oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-immune-ozone_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-immune-ozone-38">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">Carotenoids</a> in photosynthetic organisms (and possibly animals) play a major role in absorbing energy from <a href="/wiki/Singlet_oxygen" title="Singlet oxygen">singlet oxygen</a> and converting it to the unexcited ground state before it can cause harm to tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allotropes">Allotropes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_oxygen" title="Allotropes of oxygen">Allotropes of oxygen</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oxygen_molecule.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="255" data-file-width="290" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Oxygen_molecule.png/200px-Oxygen_molecule.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Oxygen_molecule.png 1.5x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oxygen_molecule.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Space-filling_model" title="Space-filling model">Space-filling model</a> representation of dioxygen (O<sub>2</sub>) molecule</div></div></div>
<p>The common <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotrope</a> of elemental oxygen on Earth is called <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_oxygen" title="Allotropes of oxygen">dioxygen</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, the major part of the Earth's atmospheric oxygen (see <a href="#Occurrence">Occurrence</a>). O<sub>2</sub> has a bond length of 121 <a href="/wiki/Picometre" title="Picometre">pm</a> and a bond energy of 498 <a href="/wiki/Joule_per_mole" title="Joule per mole">kJ/mol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> O<sub>2</sub> is used by complex forms of life, such as animals, in <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>. Other aspects of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> are covered in the remainder of this article.
</p><p>Trioxygen (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) is usually known as <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> and is a very reactive allotrope of oxygen that is damaging to lung tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuideElem48_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuideElem48-41">[38]</a></sup> Ozone is produced in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_atmosphere" title="Upper atmosphere">upper atmosphere</a> when <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> combines with atomic oxygen made by the splitting of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> by <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> (UV) radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mellor_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-17">[17]</a></sup> Since ozone absorbs strongly in the UV region of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">spectrum</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> of the upper atmosphere functions as a protective radiation shield for the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mellor_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-17">[17]</a></sup> Near the Earth's surface, it is a <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">pollutant</a> formed as a by-product of <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_system" title="Exhaust system">automobile exhaust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuideElem48_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-GuideElem48-41">[38]</a></sup> At <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_earth_orbit" title="Low earth orbit">low earth orbit</a> altitudes, sufficient atomic oxygen is present to cause <a href="/wiki/Corrosion_in_space" title="Corrosion in space">corrosion of spacecraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metastability_in_molecules" title="Metastability in molecules">metastable</a> molecule <a href="/wiki/Tetraoxygen" title="Tetraoxygen">tetraoxygen</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) was discovered in 2001,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o4_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-o4-43">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newform_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-newform-44">[41]</a></sup> and was assumed to exist in one of the six phases of <a href="/wiki/Solid_oxygen" title="Solid oxygen">solid oxygen</a>. It was proven in 2006 that this phase, created by pressurizing <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> to 20 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">GPa</a>, is in fact a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhombohedral" title="Rhombohedral">rhombohedral</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluster_chemistry" title="Cluster chemistry">cluster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup> This cluster has the potential to be a much more powerful <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">oxidizer</a> than either <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> or <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> and may therefore be used in <a href="/wiki/Rocket_propellant" title="Rocket propellant">rocket fuel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o4_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-o4-43">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newform_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-newform-44">[41]</a></sup> A metallic phase was discovered in 1990 when solid oxygen is subjected to a pressure of above 96 GPa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> and it was shown in 1998 that at very low temperatures, this phase becomes <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconducting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oxygen_discharge_tube.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Oxygen_discharge_tube.jpg/220px-Oxygen_discharge_tube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Oxygen_discharge_tube.jpg/330px-Oxygen_discharge_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Oxygen_discharge_tube.jpg/440px-Oxygen_discharge_tube.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oxygen_discharge_tube.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oxygen discharge (spectrum) tube</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">Liquid oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solid_oxygen" title="Solid oxygen">solid oxygen</a></div>
<p>Oxygen <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">dissolves</a> more readily in water than in nitrogen, and in freshwater more readily than in seawater. Water in equilibrium with air contains approximately 1 molecule of dissolved <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> for every 2 molecules of <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> (1:2), compared with an atmospheric ratio of approximately 1:4. The solubility of oxygen in water is temperature-dependent, and about twice as much (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6998146000000000000♠"></span>14.6 <abbr title="milligrams per liter">mg/L</abbr></span>) dissolves at 0 °C than at 20 °C (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6997760000000000000♠"></span>7.6 <abbr title="milligrams per liter">mg/L</abbr></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB299_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-NBB299-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> At 25 °C and 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">standard atmosphere</a> (101.3 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">kPa</a>) of air, freshwater can dissolve about 6.04 <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">milliliters</a> (mL) of oxygen per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liter" title="Liter">liter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a> contains about 4.95 mL per liter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> At 5 °C the solubility increases to 9.0 mL (50% more than at 25 °C) per liter for freshwater and 7.2 mL (45% more) per liter for sea water.
</p>

<p>Oxygen condenses at 90.20 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a> (−182.95 °C, −297.31 °F) and freezes at 54.36 K (−218.79 °C, −361.82 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solid_oxygen" title="Solid oxygen">solid</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> are clear substances with a light <a href="/wiki/Diffuse_sky_radiation" title="Diffuse sky radiation">sky-blue</a> color caused by absorption in the red (in contrast with the blue color of the sky, which is due to <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a> of blue light). High-purity liquid <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is usually obtained by the <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a> of liquefied air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> Liquid oxygen may also be condensed from air using <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a> as a coolant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-52">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Liquid oxygen is a highly reactive substance and must be segregated from combustible materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-52">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The spectroscopy of molecular oxygen is associated with the atmospheric processes of <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">aurora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Airglow" title="Airglow">airglow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krupenie1972_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krupenie1972-53">[50]</a></sup> The absorption in the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Herzberg_continuum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Herzberg continuum (page does not exist)">Herzberg continuum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Schumann%E2%80%93Runge_bands" title="Schumann–Runge bands">Schumann–Runge bands</a> in the ultraviolet produces atomic oxygen that is important in the chemistry of the middle atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrasseurSolomon2006_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrasseurSolomon2006-54">[51]</a></sup> Excited-state singlet molecular oxygen is responsible for red chemiluminescence in solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kearns1971_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kearns1971-55">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes_and_stellar_origin">Isotopes and stellar origin</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_oxygen" title="Isotopes of oxygen">Isotopes of oxygen</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg"><img alt="A concentric-sphere diagram, showing, from the core to the outer shell, iron, silicon, oxygen, neon, carbon, helium and hydrogen layers." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg/220px-Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg/330px-Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg/440px-Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Late in a massive star's life, <sup>16</sup>O concentrates in the O-shell, <sup>17</sup>O in the H-shell and <sup>18</sup>O in the He-shell.</div></div></div>
<p>Naturally occurring oxygen is composed of three stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-16" title="Oxygen-16"><sup>16</sup>O</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-17" title="Oxygen-17"><sup>17</sup>O</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-18" title="Oxygen-18"><sup>18</sup>O</a>, with <sup>16</sup>O being the most abundant (99.762% <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundance</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnvChem-Iso_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-EnvChem-Iso-56">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most <sup>16</sup>O is <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">synthesized</a> at the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helium_fusion" title="Helium fusion">helium fusion</a> process in massive <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> but some is made in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neon_burning_process" title="Neon burning process">neon burning process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer2005_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer2005-57">[54]</a></sup> <sup>17</sup>O is primarily made by the burning of hydrogen into <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> during the <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a>, making it a common isotope in the hydrogen burning zones of stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer2005_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer2005-57">[54]</a></sup> Most <sup>18</sup>O is produced when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen-14" title="Nitrogen-14"><sup>14</sup>N</a> (made abundant from CNO burning) captures a <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4"><sup>4</sup>He</a> nucleus, making <sup>18</sup>O common in the helium-rich zones of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution#Massive_stars" title="Stellar evolution">evolved, massive stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer2005_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer2005-57">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fourteen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> have been characterized. The most stable are <sup>15</sup>O with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 122.24 seconds and <sup>14</sup>O with a half-life of 70.606 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnvChem-Iso_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-EnvChem-Iso-56">[53]</a></sup> All of the remaining <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> isotopes have half-lives that are less than 27 s and the majority of these have half-lives that are less than 83 milliseconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnvChem-Iso_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-EnvChem-Iso-56">[53]</a></sup> The most common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_mode" title="Decay mode">decay mode</a> of the isotopes lighter than <sup>16</sup>O is <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">β<sup>+</sup> decay</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUDAT-13O_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-NUDAT-13O-58">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUDAT-14O_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-NUDAT-14O-59">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUDAT-15O_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-NUDAT-15O-60">[57]</a></sup> to yield nitrogen, and the most common mode for the isotopes heavier than <sup>18</sup>O is <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> to yield <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnvChem-Iso_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-EnvChem-Iso-56">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
