</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Oxygen is the most abundant chemical element by mass in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>, air, sea and land. Oxygen is the third most abundant chemical element in the universe, after hydrogen and helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB297_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBB297-62">[59]</a></sup> About 0.9% of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>'s mass is oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-ECE500-14">[14]</a></sup> Oxygen constitutes 49.2% of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a> by mass<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanl_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-63">[60]</a></sup> as part of oxide compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> and is the most abundant element by mass in the <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)#Earth's_crust_and_mantle" title="Crust (geology)">Earth's crust</a>. It is also the major component of the world's oceans (88.8% by mass).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-ECE500-14">[14]</a></sup> Oxygen gas is the second most common component of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>, taking up 20.8% of its volume and 23.1% of its mass (some 10<sup>15</sup> tonnes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE500_14-8"><a href="#cite_note-ECE500-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB298_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBB298-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[d]</a></sup> Earth is unusual among the planets of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> in having such a high concentration of oxygen gas in its atmosphere: <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> (with 0.1% <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> by volume) and <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> have much less. The <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> surrounding those planets is produced solely by the action of ultraviolet radiation on oxygen-containing molecules such as carbon dioxide.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png"><img alt="World map showing that the sea-surface oxygen is depleted around the equator and increases towards the poles." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2217" data-file-width="3158" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png/220px-WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png/330px-WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png/440px-WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WOA09_sea-surf_O2_AYool.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cold water holds more dissolved <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.</div></div></div>
<p>The unusually high concentration of oxygen gas on Earth is the result of the <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_cycle" title="Oxygen cycle">oxygen cycle</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical cycle</a> describes the movement of oxygen within and between its three main reservoirs on Earth: the atmosphere, the biosphere, and the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>. The main driving factor of the oxygen cycle is <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, which is responsible for modern Earth's atmosphere. Photosynthesis releases oxygen into the atmosphere, while <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decay</a>, and combustion remove it from the atmosphere. In the present equilibrium, production and consumption occur at the same rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Free oxygen also occurs in solution in the world's water bodies. The increased solubility of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> at lower temperatures (see <a href="#Physical_properties">Physical properties</a>) has important implications for ocean life, as polar oceans support a much higher density of life due to their higher oxygen content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">Water polluted</a> with plant nutrients such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a> or <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphates</a> may stimulate growth of algae by a process called <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> and the decay of these organisms and other biomaterials may reduce the <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> content in eutrophic water bodies. Scientists assess this aspect of water quality by measuring the water's <a href="/wiki/Biochemical_oxygen_demand" title="Biochemical oxygen demand">biochemical oxygen demand</a>, or the amount of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> needed to restore it to a normal concentration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBB301-68">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis">Analysis</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png"><img alt="Time evolution of oxygen-18 concentration on the scale of 500 million years showing many local peaks." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="726" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png/250px-Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png/375px-Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png/500px-Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_Climate_Change.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>500 million years of <a href="/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change" title="Climate variability and change">climate change</a> vs. <sup>18</sup>O</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">Paleoclimatologists</a> measure the ratio of oxygen-18 and oxygen-16 in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_shell" title="Animal shell">shells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletons</a> of marine organisms to determine the climate millions of years ago (see <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_isotope_ratio_cycle" title="Oxygen isotope ratio cycle">oxygen isotope ratio cycle</a>). <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">Seawater</a> molecules that contain the lighter <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a>, oxygen-16, evaporate at a slightly faster rate than water molecules containing the 12% heavier oxygen-18, and this disparity increases at lower temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB304_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBB304-69">[65]</a></sup> During periods of lower global temperatures, snow and rain from that evaporated water tends to be higher in oxygen-16, and the seawater left behind tends to be higher in oxygen-18. Marine organisms then incorporate more oxygen-18 into their skeletons and shells than they would in a warmer climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB304_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-NBB304-69">[65]</a></sup> Paleoclimatologists also directly measure this ratio in the water molecules of <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice core</a> samples as old as hundreds of thousands of years.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Geology_of_solar_terrestrial_planets" title="Geology of solar terrestrial planets">Planetary geologists</a> have measured the relative quantities of oxygen isotopes in samples from the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>, but were long unable to obtain reference values for the isotope ratios in the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, believed to be the same as those of the <a href="/wiki/Nebular_hypothesis" title="Nebular hypothesis">primordial solar nebula</a>. Analysis of a <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> wafer exposed to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> in space and returned by the crashed <a href="/wiki/Genesis_(spacecraft)" title="Genesis (spacecraft)">Genesis spacecraft</a> has shown that the Sun has a higher proportion of oxygen-16 than does the Earth. The measurement implies that an unknown process depleted oxygen-16 from the Sun's <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">disk of protoplanetary material</a> prior to the coalescence of dust grains that formed the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Oxygen presents two spectrophotometric <a href="/wiki/Absorption_band" title="Absorption band">absorption bands</a> peaking at the wavelengths 687 and 760 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>. Some <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a> scientists have proposed using the measurement of the radiance coming from vegetation canopies in those bands to characterize plant health status from a <a href="/wiki/Earth_observation_satellite" title="Earth observation satellite">satellite</a> platform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup> This approach exploits the fact that in those bands it is possible to discriminate the vegetation's <a href="/wiki/Reflectance" title="Reflectance">reflectance</a> from its <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescence</a>, which is much weaker. The measurement is technically difficult owing to the low <a href="/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Signal-to-noise ratio">signal-to-noise ratio</a> and the physical structure of vegetation; but it has been proposed as a possible method of monitoring the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a> from satellites on a global scale.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_production_and_role_of_O2">Biological production and role of O<sub>2</sub></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dioxygen_in_biological_reactions" title="Dioxygen in biological reactions">Dioxygen in biological reactions</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photosynthesis_and_respiration">Photosynthesis and respiration</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg"><img alt="A diagram of photosynthesis processes, including income of water and carbon dioxide, illumination and release of oxygen. Reactions produce ATP and NADPH in a Calvin cycle with a sugar as a by product." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1027" data-file-width="830" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/220px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/330px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/440px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photosynthesis splits water to liberate <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and fixes <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> into sugar in what is called a <a href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" title="Calvin cycle">Calvin cycle</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In nature, free oxygen is produced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">light-driven splitting</a> of water during oxygenic <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. According to some estimates, <a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">green algae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> in marine environments provide about 70% of the free oxygen produced on Earth, and the rest is produced by terrestrial plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup> Other estimates of the oceanic contribution to atmospheric oxygen are higher, while some estimates are lower, suggesting oceans produce ~45% of Earth's atmospheric oxygen each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>A simplified overall formula for photosynthesis is<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>6 CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> + 6 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> + <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> → <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> + 6 <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>or simply
</p>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> + water + sunlight → <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> + dioxygen</dd></dl>
<p>Photolytic <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_evolution" title="Oxygen evolution">oxygen evolution</a> occurs in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thylakoid_membrane" title="Thylakoid membrane">thylakoid membranes</a> of photosynthetic organisms and requires the energy of four <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[e]</a></sup> Many steps are involved, but the result is the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> gradient across the thylakoid membrane, which is used to synthesize <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP) via <a href="/wiki/Photophosphorylation" title="Photophosphorylation">photophosphorylation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raven-76">[71]</a></sup> The <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> remaining (after production of the water molecule) is released into the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[f]</a></sup>
</p><p>Oxygen is used in <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondria</a> in the generation of ATP during <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">oxidative phosphorylation</a>. The reaction for aerobic respiration is essentially the reverse of photosynthesis and is simplified as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> + 6 <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → 6 CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> + 6 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> + 2880 kJ/mol</dd></dl>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffuses</a> through membranes in the lungs and into <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">Hemoglobin</a> binds <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, changing color from bluish red to bright red<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuideElem48_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-GuideElem48-41">[38]</a></sup> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is released from another part of hemoglobin through the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_effect" title="Bohr effect">Bohr effect</a>). Other animals use <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">molluscs</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Hemerythrin" title="Hemerythrin">hemerythrin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">lobsters</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB298_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-NBB298-64">[61]</a></sup> A liter of blood can dissolve 200 cm<sup>3</sup> of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB298_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-NBB298-64">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic</a> <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">metazoa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20370908_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20370908-78">[72]</a></sup> oxygen was thought to be a requirement for all complex life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">Reactive oxygen species</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">superoxide</a> ion (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), are reactive by-products of oxygen use in organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB298_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-NBB298-64">[61]</a></sup> Parts of the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> of higher organisms create peroxide, superoxide, and singlet oxygen to destroy invading microbes. Reactive oxygen species also play an important role in the <a href="/wiki/Hypersensitive_response" title="Hypersensitive response">hypersensitive response</a> of plants against pathogen attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raven-76">[71]</a></sup> Oxygen is damaging to <a href="/wiki/Obligate_anaerobe" title="Obligate anaerobe">obligately anaerobic organisms</a>, which were the dominant form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">early life</a> on Earth until <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> began to accumulate in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere</a> about 2.5 billion years ago during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Oxygenation_Event" title="Great Oxygenation Event">Great Oxygenation Event</a>, about a billion years after the first appearance of these organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131003_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131003-81">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>An adult human at rest <a href="/wiki/Breathing" title="Breathing">inhales</a> 1.8 to 2.4 grams of oxygen per minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[76]</a></sup> This amounts to more than 6 billion tonnes of oxygen inhaled by humanity per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[g]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Living_organisms">Living organisms</span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="partial_pressure"></span>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-left:25px">

</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KPa" title="KPa">kPa</a></td>
<td>14.2</td>
<td>11<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmHg_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-mmHg-84">[77]</a></sup>-13<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmHg_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-mmHg-84">[77]</a></sup></td>

</td></tr>
