<p>The free oxygen <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">partial pressure</a> in the body of a living vertebrate organism is highest in the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory system</a>, and decreases along any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_system" title="Arterial system">arterial system</a>, peripheral tissues, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venous_system" title="Venous system">venous system</a>, respectively. Partial pressure is the pressure that oxygen would have if it alone occupied the volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Build-up_in_the_atmosphere">Build-up in the atmosphere</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_oxygen" title="Geological history of oxygen">Geological history of oxygen</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oxygenation-atm.svg"><img alt="A graph showing time evolution of oxygen pressure on Earth; the pressure increases from zero to 0.2 atmospheres." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="840" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Oxygenation-atm.svg/300px-Oxygenation-atm.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Oxygenation-atm.svg/450px-Oxygenation-atm.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Oxygenation-atm.svg/600px-Oxygenation-atm.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oxygenation-atm.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> build-up in Earth's atmosphere: 1) no <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> produced; 2) <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> produced, but absorbed in oceans &amp; seabed rock; 3) <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> starts to gas out of the oceans, but is absorbed by land surfaces and formation of ozone layer; 4–5) <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> sinks filled and the gas accumulates</div></div></div>
<p>Free oxygen gas was almost nonexistent in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a> before photosynthetic <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> evolved, probably about 3.5 billion years ago. Free oxygen first appeared in significant quantities during the <a href="/wiki/Paleoproterozoic" title="Paleoproterozoic">Paleoproterozoic</a> eon (between 3.0 and 2.3 billion years ago).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crowe2013_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crowe2013-88">[81]</a></sup> Even if there was much dissolved <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> in the oceans when oxygenic photosynthesis was getting more common, it appears the <a href="/wiki/Banded_iron_formation" title="Banded iron formation">banded iron formations</a> were created by anoxyenic or micro-aerophilic iron-oxidizing bacteria which dominated the deeper areas of the <a href="/wiki/Photic_zone" title="Photic zone">photic zone</a>, while oxygen-producing cyanobacteria covered the shallows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[82]</a></sup> Free oxygen began to <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgas</a> from the oceans 3–2.7 billion years ago, reaching 10% of its present level around 1.7 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crowe2013_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crowe2013-88">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-90">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The presence of large amounts of dissolved and free oxygen in the oceans and atmosphere may have driven most of the extant <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic organisms</a> to <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Oxygenation_Event" title="Great Oxygenation Event">Great Oxygenation Event</a> (<i>oxygen catastrophe</i>) about 2.4 billion years ago. <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">Cellular respiration</a> using <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> enables <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic organisms</a> to produce much more <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a> than anaerobic organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman-91">[84]</a></sup> Cellular respiration of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> occurs in all <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>, including all complex multicellular organisms such as plants and animals.
</p><p>Since the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> period 540 million years ago, atmospheric <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> levels have fluctuated between 15% and 30% by volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geologic_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-geologic-92">[85]</a></sup> Towards the end of the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> period (about 300 million years ago) atmospheric <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> levels reached a maximum of 35% by volume,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geologic_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-geologic-92">[85]</a></sup> which may have contributed to the large size of insects and amphibians at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butterfield2009_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butterfield2009-93">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Variations in atmospheric oxygen concentration have shaped past climates. When oxygen declined, atmospheric density dropped, which in turn increased surface evaporation, causing precipitation increases and warmer temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the current rate of photosynthesis it would take about 2,000 years to regenerate the entire <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> in the present atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_free_oxygen">Extraterrestrial free oxygen</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_atmosphere" title="Extraterrestrial atmosphere">Extraterrestrial atmosphere</a></div>
<p>In the field of <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a> and in the search for <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> oxygen is a strong <a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignature</a>. That said it might not be a definite biosignature, being <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_atmosphere#Abiotic_oxygen" title="Extraterrestrial atmosphere">possibly produced abiotically</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_bodies" title="Celestial bodies">celestial bodies</a> with processes and conditions (such as a peculiar <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>) which allow free oxygen,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[91]</a></sup> like with <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede's</a> thin
oxygen atmospheres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall1998_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hall1998-99">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_production">Industrial production</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Air_separation" title="Air separation">Air separation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_evolution" title="Oxygen evolution">Oxygen evolution</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">Fractional distillation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg"><img alt="A drawing of three vertical pipes connected at the bottom and filled with oxygen (left pipe), water (middle) and hydrogen (right). Anode and cathode electrodes are inserted into the left and right pipes and externally connected to a battery." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="209" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg/170px-Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg/255px-Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg/340px-Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hofmann_voltameter_fr.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hofmann_voltameter" title="Hofmann voltameter">Hofmann electrolysis apparatus</a> used in electrolysis of water.</div></div></div>
<p>One hundred million tonnes of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> are extracted from air for industrial uses annually by two primary methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-NBB300-15">[15]</a></sup> The most common method is <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a> of liquefied air, with <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distilling</a> as a vapor while <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is left as a liquid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-NBB300-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The other primary method of producing <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is passing a stream of clean, dry air through one bed of a pair of identical <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">zeolite</a> molecular sieves, which absorbs the nitrogen and delivers a gas stream that is 90% to 93% <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-NBB300-15">[15]</a></sup> Simultaneously, nitrogen gas is released from the other nitrogen-saturated zeolite bed, by reducing the chamber operating pressure and diverting part of the oxygen gas from the producer bed through it, in the reverse direction of flow. After a set cycle time the operation of the two beds is interchanged, thereby allowing for a continuous supply of gaseous oxygen to be pumped through a pipeline. This is known as <a href="/wiki/Pressure_swing_adsorption" title="Pressure swing adsorption">pressure swing adsorption</a>. Oxygen gas is increasingly obtained by these non-<a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">cryogenic</a> technologies (see also the related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vacuum_swing_adsorption" title="Vacuum swing adsorption">vacuum swing adsorption</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Oxygen gas can also be produced through <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a> into molecular oxygen and hydrogen. DC electricity must be used: if AC is used, the gases in each limb consist of hydrogen and oxygen in the explosive ratio 2:1. A similar method is the electrocatalytic <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> evolution from oxides and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxoacid" title="Oxoacid">oxoacids</a>. Chemical catalysts can be used as well, such as in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oxygen_generator" title="Chemical oxygen generator">chemical oxygen generators</a> or oxygen candles that are used as part of the life-support equipment on submarines, and are still part of standard equipment on commercial airliners in case of depressurization emergencies. Another air separation method is forcing air to dissolve through <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> membranes based on <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_dioxide" title="Zirconium dioxide">zirconium dioxide</a> by either high pressure or an electric current, to produce nearly pure <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-NBB301-68">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage">Storage</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Compressed_gas_cylinders.mapp_and_oxygen.triddle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1609" data-file-width="1517" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Compressed_gas_cylinders.mapp_and_oxygen.triddle.jpg/220px-Compressed_gas_cylinders.mapp_and_oxygen.triddle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Compressed_gas_cylinders.mapp_and_oxygen.triddle.jpg/330px-Compressed_gas_cylinders.mapp_and_oxygen.triddle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Compressed_gas_cylinders.mapp_and_oxygen.triddle.jpg/440px-Compressed_gas_cylinders.mapp_and_oxygen.triddle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Compressed_gas_cylinders.mapp_and_oxygen.triddle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oxygen and <a href="/wiki/MAPP_gas" title="MAPP gas">MAPP gas</a> compressed-gas cylinders with regulators</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Oxygen_storage" title="Oxygen storage">Oxygen storage</a> methods include high-pressure <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_tank" title="Oxygen tank">oxygen tanks</a>, cryogenics and chemical compounds. For reasons of economy, oxygen is often transported in bulk as a liquid in specially insulated tankers, since one <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">liter</a> of liquefied oxygen is equivalent to 840 liters of gaseous oxygen at atmospheric pressure and 20 °C (68 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-NBB300-15">[15]</a></sup> Such tankers are used to refill bulk liquid-oxygen storage containers, which stand outside hospitals and other institutions that need large volumes of pure oxygen gas. Liquid oxygen is passed through <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchangers</a>, which convert the cryogenic liquid into gas before it enters the building. Oxygen is also stored and shipped in smaller cylinders containing the compressed gas; a form that is useful in certain portable medical applications and <a href="/wiki/Oxy-fuel_welding_and_cutting" title="Oxy-fuel welding and cutting">oxy-fuel welding and cutting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB300_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-NBB300-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Breathing_gas" title="Breathing gas">Breathing gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">Redox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">Combustion</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical">Medical</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg"><img alt="A gray device with a label DeVILBISS LT4000 and some text on the front panel. A green plastic pipe is running from the device." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="1224" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg/170px-Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg/255px-Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg/340px-Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Home_oxygen_concentrator.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_concentrator" title="Oxygen concentrator">oxygen concentrator</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Emphysema" title="Emphysema">emphysema</a> patient's house</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_therapy" title="Oxygen therapy">Oxygen therapy</a></div>
<p>Uptake of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> from the air is the essential purpose of <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">respiration</a>, so oxygen supplementation is used in <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>. Treatment not only increases oxygen levels in the patient's blood, but has the secondary effect of decreasing resistance to blood flow in many types of diseased lungs, easing work load on the heart. <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_therapy" title="Oxygen therapy">Oxygen therapy</a> is used to treat <a href="/wiki/Emphysema" title="Emphysema">emphysema</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, some heart disorders (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congestive_heart_failure" title="Congestive heart failure">congestive heart failure</a>), some disorders that cause increased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery_pressure" title="Pulmonary artery pressure">pulmonary artery pressure</a>, and any <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a> that impairs the body's ability to take up and use gaseous oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE510_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE510-101">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Treatments are flexible enough to be used in hospitals, the patient's home, or increasingly by portable devices. <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_tent" title="Oxygen tent">Oxygen tents</a> were once commonly used in oxygen supplementation, but have since been replaced mostly by the use of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_mask" title="Oxygen mask">oxygen masks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nasal_cannula" title="Nasal cannula">nasal cannulas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18540928_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18540928-102">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_medicine" title="Hyperbaric medicine">Hyperbaric</a> (high-pressure) medicine uses special <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_oxygen_chamber" title="Hyperbaric oxygen chamber">oxygen chambers</a> to increase the <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">partial pressure</a> of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> around the patient and, when needed, the medical staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8931286_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8931286-103">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">Carbon monoxide poisoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas_gangrene" title="Gas gangrene">gas gangrene</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a> (the 'bends') are sometimes addressed with this therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[97]</a></sup> Increased <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> concentration in the lungs helps to displace <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> from the heme group of <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15233173_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15233173-106">[99]</a></sup> Oxygen gas is poisonous to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_bacteria" title="Anaerobic bacteria">anaerobic bacteria</a> that cause gas gangrene, so increasing its partial pressure helps kill them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[101]</a></sup> Decompression sickness occurs in divers who decompress too quickly after a dive, resulting in bubbles of inert gas, mostly nitrogen and helium, forming in the blood. Increasing the pressure of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> as soon as possible helps to redissolve the bubbles back into the blood so that these excess gasses can be exhaled naturally through the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE510_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-ECE510-101">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[103]</a></sup> Normobaric oxygen administration at the highest available concentration is frequently used as first aid for any diving injury that may involve inert gas bubble formation in the tissues. There is epidemiological support for its use from a statistical study of cases recorded in a long term database.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Longphre_et_al_2007_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Longphre_et_al_2007-111">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emergency_O2_for_scuba_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emergency_O2_for_scuba-112">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAN_Europe_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAN_Europe-113">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_support_and_recreational_use">Life support and recreational use</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS057-89-067_-_Wisoff_on_the_Arm_(Retouched).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5230" data-file-width="5230" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/STS057-89-067_-_Wisoff_on_the_Arm_%28Retouched%29.jpg/220px-STS057-89-067_-_Wisoff_on_the_Arm_%28Retouched%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/STS057-89-067_-_Wisoff_on_the_Arm_%28Retouched%29.jpg/330px-STS057-89-067_-_Wisoff_on_the_Arm_%28Retouched%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/STS057-89-067_-_Wisoff_on_the_Arm_%28Retouched%29.jpg/440px-STS057-89-067_-_Wisoff_on_the_Arm_%28Retouched%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS057-89-067_-_Wisoff_on_the_Arm_(Retouched).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Low pressure pure <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is used in <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">space suits</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>An application of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> as a low-pressure <a href="/wiki/Breathing_gas" title="Breathing gas">breathing gas</a> is in modern <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">space suits</a>, which surround their occupant's body with the breathing gas. These devices use nearly pure oxygen at about one-third normal pressure, resulting in a normal blood partial pressure of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. This trade-off of higher oxygen concentration for lower pressure is needed to maintain suit flexibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11541018_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11541018-114">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2730484_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid2730484-115">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">Scuba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surface-supplied_diving" title="Surface-supplied diving">surface-supplied</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Underwater_divers" title="Underwater divers">underwater divers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submariners</a> also rely on artificially delivered <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. Submarines, submersibles and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_diving_suits" title="Atmospheric diving suits">atmospheric diving suits</a> usually operate at normal atmospheric pressure. Breathing air is scrubbed of carbon dioxide by chemical extraction and oxygen is replaced to maintain a constant partial pressure. <a href="/wiki/Ambient_pressure" title="Ambient pressure">Ambient pressure</a> divers breathe air or gas mixtures with an oxygen fraction suited to the operating depth. Pure or nearly pure <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> use in diving at pressures higher than atmospheric is usually limited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rebreathers" title="Rebreathers">rebreathers</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Decompression_(diving)" title="Decompression (diving)">decompression</a> at relatively shallow depths (~6 meters depth, or less),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acott_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Acott-116">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Longphre_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Longphre-117">[110]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_treatment_schedules" title="Hyperbaric treatment schedules">medical treatment in recompression chambers</a> at pressures up to 2.8 bar, where acute oxygen toxicity can be managed without the risk of drowning. Deeper diving requires significant dilution of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> with other gases, such as nitrogen or helium, to prevent <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_toxicity" title="Oxygen toxicity">oxygen toxicity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acott_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Acott-116">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>People who climb mountains or fly in non-pressurized <a href="/wiki/Fixed-wing_aircraft" title="Fixed-wing aircraft">fixed-wing aircraft</a> sometimes have supplemental <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[h]</a></sup> Pressurized commercial airplanes have an emergency supply of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> automatically supplied to the passengers in case of cabin depressurization. Sudden cabin pressure loss activates <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oxygen_generator" title="Chemical oxygen generator">chemical oxygen generators</a> above each seat, causing <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_mask" title="Oxygen mask">oxygen masks</a> to drop. Pulling on the masks "to start the flow of oxygen" as cabin safety instructions dictate, forces iron filings into the <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chlorate" title="Sodium chlorate">sodium chlorate</a> inside the canister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-NBB301-68">[64]</a></sup> A steady stream of oxygen gas is then produced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a> reaction.
</p><p>Oxygen, as a mild <a href="/wiki/Euphoria" title="Euphoria">euphoric</a>, has a history of recreational use in <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_bar" title="Oxygen bar">oxygen bars</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Sport" title="Sport">sports</a>. Oxygen bars are establishments found in the United States since the late 1990s that offer higher than normal <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> exposure for a minimal fee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA-O2Bars_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDA-O2Bars-119">[111]</a></sup> Professional athletes, especially in <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American football</a>, sometimes go off-field between plays to don oxygen masks to boost performance. The pharmacological effect is doubted; a <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a> effect is a more likely explanation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA-O2Bars_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FDA-O2Bars-119">[111]</a></sup> Available studies support a performance boost from oxygen enriched mixtures only if it is inhaled <i>during</i> <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_exercise" title="Aerobic exercise">aerobic exercise</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other recreational uses that do not involve breathing include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrotechnic" title="Pyrotechnic">pyrotechnic</a> applications, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Goble" title="George Goble">George Goble</a>'s five-second ignition of <a href="/wiki/Barbecue" title="Barbecue">barbecue</a> grills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial">Industrial</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clabecq_JPG01.jpg"><img alt="An elderly worker in a helmet is facing his side to the viewer in an industrial hall. The hall is dark but is illuminated yellow glowing splashes of a melted substance." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="616" data-file-width="825" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Clabecq_JPG01.jpg/220px-Clabecq_JPG01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Clabecq_JPG01.jpg/330px-Clabecq_JPG01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Clabecq_JPG01.jpg/440px-Clabecq_JPG01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clabecq_JPG01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Most commercially produced <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is used to <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelt</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Decarburization" title="Decarburization">decarburize</a> <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">Smelting</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a> into <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> consumes 55% of commercially produced oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-NBB301-68">[64]</a></sup> In this process, <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is injected through a high-pressure lance into molten iron, which removes <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> impurities and excess <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> as the respective oxides, <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. The reactions are <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">exothermic</a>, so the temperature increases to 1,700 °<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">C</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-NBB301-68">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another 25% of commercially produced oxygen is used by the chemical industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_68-5"><a href="#cite_note-NBB301-68">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">Ethylene</a> is reacted with <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> to create <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">ethylene oxide</a>, which, in turn, is converted into <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_glycol" title="Ethylene glycol">ethylene glycol</a>; the primary feeder material used to manufacture a host of products, including <a href="/wiki/Antifreeze" title="Antifreeze">antifreeze</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polyester" title="Polyester">polyester</a> polymers (the precursors of many <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabric" title="Fabric">fabrics</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_68-6"><a href="#cite_note-NBB301-68">[64]</a></sup> Large quantities of oxygen or air is used in oxy-cracking process<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[114]</a></sup> and for the production of acrylic acid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[115]</a></sup> diformyl-furane,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[116]</a></sup> and benzylic acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[117]</a></sup> On the other hand, the electrochemical synthesis of hydrogen peroxide from oxygen is a promising technology to replace the currently used hydroquinone-process. Last but not least, catalytic oxidation is used in afterburners to get rid of hazardous gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the remaining 20% of commercially produced oxygen is used in medical applications, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_welding" title="Gas welding">metal cutting and welding</a>, as an oxidizer in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_fuel" title="Rocket fuel">rocket fuel</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Water_treatment" title="Water treatment">water treatment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBB301_68-7"><a href="#cite_note-NBB301-68">[64]</a></sup> Oxygen is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxyacetylene_welding" title="Oxyacetylene welding">oxyacetylene welding</a>, burning <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a> with <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> to produce a very hot flame. In this process, metal up to 60 cm (24 in) thick is first heated with a small oxy-acetylene flame and then quickly cut by a large stream of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE508_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE508-128">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compounds_of_oxygen" title="Compounds of oxygen">Compounds of oxygen</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg"><img alt="Water flowing from a bottle into a glass." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="404" data-file-width="298" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg/170px-Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg/255px-Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stilles_Mineralwasser.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">Water</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>) is the most familiar oxygen compound.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> of oxygen is −2 in almost all known compounds of oxygen. The oxidation state −1 is found in a few compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">peroxides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup> Compounds containing oxygen in other oxidation states are very uncommon: −1/2 (<a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">superoxides</a>), −1/3 (<a href="/wiki/Ozonide" title="Ozonide">ozonides</a>), 0 (<a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_oxygen" title="Allotropes of oxygen">elemental</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypofluorous_acid" title="Hypofluorous acid">hypofluorous acid</a>), +1/2 (<a href="/wiki/Dioxygenyl" title="Dioxygenyl">dioxygenyl</a>), +1 (<a href="/wiki/Dioxygen_difluoride" title="Dioxygen difluoride">dioxygen difluoride</a>), and +2 (<a href="/wiki/Oxygen_difluoride" title="Oxygen difluoride">oxygen difluoride</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides_and_other_inorganic_compounds">Oxides and other inorganic compounds</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">Water</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>) is an oxide of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and the most familiar oxygen compound. Hydrogen atoms are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalent_bonding" title="Covalent bonding">covalently bonded</a> to oxygen in a water molecule but also have an additional attraction (about 23.3 kJ/mol per hydrogen atom) to an adjacent oxygen atom in a separate molecule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[123]</a></sup> These <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> between water molecules hold them approximately 15% closer than what would be expected in a simple liquid with just <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force" title="Van der Waals force">van der Waals forces</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[i]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rust_screw.jpg"><img alt="A rusty piece of a bolt." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rust_screw.jpg/220px-Rust_screw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rust_screw.jpg/330px-Rust_screw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rust_screw.jpg/440px-Rust_screw.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rust_screw.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oxides, such as <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">rust</a>, form when oxygen combines with other elements.</div></div></div>
<p>Due to its <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a>, oxygen forms <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bonds</a> with almost all other elements to give corresponding <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a>. The surface of most metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, are oxidized in the presence of air and become coated with a thin film of oxide that <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivates</a> the metal and slows further <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a>. Many oxides of the <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a> are <a href="/wiki/Non-stoichiometric_compound" title="Non-stoichiometric compound">non-stoichiometric compounds</a>, with slightly less metal than the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> would show. For example, the mineral <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_oxide" title="Iron(II) oxide">FeO</a> (<a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCstite" title="Wüstite">wüstite</a>) is written as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {Fe}}_{1-x}{\ce {O}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>Fe</mtext>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>x</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>O</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {Fe}}_{1-x}{\ce {O}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {Fe}}_{1-x}{\ce {O}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f7cf86639f74d4b3d0c4fe18874ad4cba403c264" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.631ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, where <i>x</i> is usually around 0.05.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Oxygen is present in the atmosphere in trace quantities in the form of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>). The <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crustal</a> <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock</a> is composed in large part of oxides of <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> (<a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silica</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, as found in <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>), aluminium (<a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">Al<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, in <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">corundum</a>), iron (<a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron(III) oxide</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, in <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">rust</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>). The rest of the Earth's crust is also made of oxygen compounds, in particular various complex <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a> (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">silicate minerals</a>). The Earth's mantle, of much larger mass than the crust, is largely composed of silicates of magnesium and iron.
</p><p>Water-<a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">soluble</a> silicates in the form of <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Si<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> are used as <a href="/wiki/Detergent" title="Detergent">detergents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adhesive" title="Adhesive">adhesives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE507_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE507-135">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Oxygen also acts as a <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> for transition metals, forming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transition_metal_dioxygen_complexes" title="Transition metal dioxygen complexes">transition metal dioxygen complexes</a>, which feature metal–<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. This class of compounds includes the <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a> proteins <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[127]</a></sup> An exotic and unusual reaction occurs with <a href="/wiki/Platinum_hexafluoride" title="Platinum hexafluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">PtF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></a>, which oxidizes oxygen to give O<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup>PtF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, <a href="/wiki/Dioxygenyl_hexafluoroplatinate" title="Dioxygenyl hexafluoroplatinate">dioxygenyl hexafluoroplatinate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE505_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE505-137">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_compounds">Organic compounds</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acetone-3D-vdW.png"><img alt="A ball structure of a molecule. Its backbone is a zig-zag chain of three carbon atoms connected in the center to an oxygen atom and on the end to 6 hydrogens." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="958" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Acetone-3D-vdW.png/220px-Acetone-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Acetone-3D-vdW.png/330px-Acetone-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Acetone-3D-vdW.png/440px-Acetone-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acetone-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">Acetone</a> is an important feeder material in the chemical industry. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:red; color:black;"> </span> Oxygen</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:black; color:white;"> </span> Carbon</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="border: 1px solid silver;background-color:white; color:black;"> </span> Hydrogen</div></div></div></div>
<p>Among the most important classes of organic compounds that contain oxygen are (where "R" is an organic group): <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(chemistry)" title="Alcohol (chemistry)">alcohols</a> (R-OH); <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ethers</a> (R-O-R); <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a> (R-CO-R); <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a> (R-CO-H); <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acids</a> (R-COOH); <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a> (R-COO-R); <a href="/wiki/Organic_acid_anhydride" title="Organic acid anhydride">acid anhydrides</a> (R-CO-O-CO-R); and <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">amides</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">R-C(O)-NR<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>). There are many important organic <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a> that contain oxygen, including: <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isopropyl_alcohol" title="Isopropyl alcohol">isopropanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Furan" title="Furan">furan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrofuran" title="Tetrahydrofuran">THF</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">diethyl ether</a>, <a href="/wiki/1,4-Dioxane" title="1,4-Dioxane">dioxane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_acetate" title="Ethyl acetate">ethyl acetate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimethylformamide" title="Dimethylformamide">DMF</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_sulfoxide" title="Dimethyl sulfoxide">DMSO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">formic acid</a>. Acetone (<span class="chemf nowrap">(CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO</span>) and <a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenol</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>OH</span>) are used as feeder materials in the synthesis of many different substances. Other important organic compounds that contain oxygen are: <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glutaraldehyde" title="Glutaraldehyde">glutaraldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_anhydride" title="Acetic anhydride">acetic anhydride</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Acetamide" title="Acetamide">acetamide</a>. <a href="/wiki/Epoxide" title="Epoxide">Epoxides</a> are ethers in which the oxygen atom is part of a ring of three atoms. The element is similarly found in almost all <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a> that are important to (or generated by) life.
</p><p>Oxygen reacts spontaneously with many <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic</a> compounds at or below room temperature in a process called <a href="/wiki/Autoxidation" title="Autoxidation">autoxidation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE506_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE506-138">[129]</a></sup> Most of the <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> that contain oxygen are not made by direct action of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. Organic compounds important in industry and commerce that are made by direct oxidation of a precursor include <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">ethylene oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peracetic_acid" title="Peracetic acid">peracetic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE507_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-ECE507-135">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
